			<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Coal</h1>
									<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
									<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
								<div id="contentSub"></div>
												<div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
				</div>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div role="note" class="hatnote">For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Coal_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Coal (disambiguation)">Coal (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Coal_bituminous.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Coal_bituminous.jpg/220px-Coal_bituminous.jpg" width="220" height="188" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Coal_bituminous.jpg/330px-Coal_bituminous.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/20/Coal_bituminous.jpg 2x" data-file-width="360" data-file-height="308" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Coal_bituminous.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Bituminous_coal" title="Bituminous coal">Bituminous coal</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Coal</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Combustible" class="mw-redirect" title="Combustible">combustible</a> black or brownish-black <a href="/wiki/Sedimentary_rock" title="Sedimentary rock">sedimentary rock</a> usually occurring in <a href="/wiki/Stratum" title="Stratum">rock strata</a> in layers or veins called <b>coal beds</b> or <b>coal seams</b>. The harder forms, such as <a href="/wiki/Anthracite" title="Anthracite">anthracite coal</a>, can be regarded as <a href="/wiki/Metamorphic_rock" title="Metamorphic rock">metamorphic rock</a> because of later exposure to elevated temperature and <a href="/wiki/Pressure" title="Pressure">pressure</a>. Coal is composed primarily of <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>, along with variable quantities of other elements, chiefly <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuel</a>, coal forms when dead plant matter is converted into <a href="/wiki/Peat" title="Peat">peat</a>, which in turn is converted into <a href="/wiki/Lignite" title="Lignite">lignite</a>, then sub-bituminous coal, after that <a href="/wiki/Bituminous_coal" title="Bituminous coal">bituminous coal</a>, and lastly <a href="/wiki/Anthracite" title="Anthracite">anthracite</a>. This involves biological and geological processes that take place over time.</p>
<p>Throughout history, coal has been used as an energy resource, primarily burned for the production of electricity and heat, and is also used for industrial purposes, such as refining metals. Coal is the largest source of energy for the <a href="/wiki/Electricity_generation" title="Electricity generation">generation of electricity</a> worldwide, as well as one of the largest worldwide <a href="/wiki/Anthropogenic" class="mw-redirect" title="Anthropogenic">anthropogenic</a> sources of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> releases. The extraction of coal, its use in energy production and its byproducts are all associated with <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_the_coal_industry" title="Environmental impact of the coal industry">environmental</a> and <a href="/wiki/Coal_worker%27s_pneumoconiosis" class="mw-redirect" title="Coal worker's pneumoconiosis">health effects</a> including <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-psr_coal-damages-health_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psr_coal-damages-health-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>Coal is extracted from the ground by <a href="/wiki/Coal_mining" title="Coal mining">coal mining</a>. Since 1983, the world's top coal producer has been <a href="/wiki/Coal_in_China" title="Coal in China">China</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BPReview2016_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BPReview2016-3">[3]</a></sup> In 2015 China produced 3,747 million tonnes of coal – 47.7% of 7,861 million tonnes world coal production. In 2015 other large producers were <a href="/wiki/Coal_mining_in_the_United_States" title="Coal mining in the United States">United States</a> (813 million tonnes), <a href="/wiki/Coal_mining_in_India" title="Coal mining in India">India</a> (678), <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> (539) and <a href="/wiki/Coal_in_Australia" title="Coal in Australia">Australia</a> (503).<sup id="cite_ref-BPReview2016_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BPReview2016-3">[3]</a></sup> In 2010 the largest exporters were Australia with 328 million tonnes (27.1% of world coal export) and Indonesia with 316 million tonnes (26.1%),<sup id="cite_ref-ei_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ei-4">[4]</a></sup> while the largest importers were Japan with 207 million tonnes (17.5% of world coal import), China with 195 million tonnes (16.6%) and South Korea with 126 million tonnes (10.7%).<sup id="cite_ref-eim_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eim-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<p>The word originally took the form <i>col</i> in <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a>, from <a href="/wiki/Proto-Germanic" class="mw-redirect" title="Proto-Germanic">Proto-Germanic</a> *<i>kula</i>(<i>n</i>), which in turn is hypothesized to come from the <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European" class="mw-redirect" title="Proto-Indo-European">Proto-Indo-European</a> root *<i>g</i>(<i>e</i>)<i>u-lo-</i> "live coal".<sup id="cite_ref-etym_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-etym-6">[6]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic</a> cognates include the <a href="/wiki/Old_Frisian" title="Old Frisian">Old Frisian</a> <i>kole</i>, <a href="/wiki/Middle_Dutch" title="Middle Dutch">Middle Dutch</a> <i>cole</i>, <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a> <i>kool</i>, <a href="/wiki/Old_High_German" title="Old High German">Old High German</a> <i>chol</i>, <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a> <i>Kohle</i> and <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Old Norse</a> <i>kol</i>, and the <a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">Irish</a> word <i>gual</i> is also a cognate via the <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European</a> root.<sup id="cite_ref-etym_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-etym-6">[6]</a></sup> In Old Turkic languages, <i>kül</i> is "ash(es), cinders", <i>öčür</i> is "quench". The compound "charcoal" in Turkic is <i>öčür(ülmüş) kül</i>, literally "quenched ashes, cinders, coals" with elided anlaut <i>ö-</i> and inflection affixes <i>-ülmüş</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>The word took on the meaning "mineral consisting of fossilized carbon" in the thirteenth century.<sup id="cite_ref-etym_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-etym-6">[6]</a></sup> Often the terms "<a href="/wiki/Coal_plant" class="mw-redirect" title="Coal plant">coal plant</a>" or "<a href="/wiki/Coal_power_plant" class="mw-redirect" title="Coal power plant">coal power plant</a>" are used, referring to the presumed origin of coal being old fossilized plants.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation">Formation</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Struktura_chemiczna_w%C4%99gla_kamiennego.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Struktura_chemiczna_w%C4%99gla_kamiennego.svg/220px-Struktura_chemiczna_w%C4%99gla_kamiennego.svg.png" width="220" height="180" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Struktura_chemiczna_w%C4%99gla_kamiennego.svg/330px-Struktura_chemiczna_w%C4%99gla_kamiennego.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Struktura_chemiczna_w%C4%99gla_kamiennego.svg/440px-Struktura_chemiczna_w%C4%99gla_kamiennego.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1100" data-file-height="900" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Struktura_chemiczna_w%C4%99gla_kamiennego.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Example chemical structure of coal</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At various times in the geologic past, the Earth had dense forests in low-lying wetland areas. Due to natural processes such as flooding, these forests were buried underneath soil. As more and more soil deposited over them, they were compressed. The temperature also rose as they sank deeper and deeper. As the process continued the plant matter was protected from <a href="/wiki/Biodegradation" title="Biodegradation">biodegradation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oxidation" class="mw-redirect" title="Oxidation">oxidation</a>, usually by mud or acidic water. This trapped the carbon in immense <a href="/wiki/Peat_bog" class="mw-redirect" title="Peat bog">peat bogs</a> that were eventually covered and deeply buried by sediments. Under high pressure and high temperature, dead vegetation was slowly converted to coal. As coal contains mainly carbon, the conversion of dead vegetation into coal is called carbonization.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>The wide, shallow seas of the <a href="/wiki/Carboniferous" title="Carboniferous">Carboniferous</a> Period provided ideal conditions for coal formation, although coal is known from most geological periods. The exception is the coal gap in the <a href="/wiki/Permian%E2%80%93Triassic_extinction_event" title="Permian–Triassic extinction event">Permian–Triassic extinction event</a>, where coal is rare. Coal is known from <a href="/wiki/Precambrian" title="Precambrian">Precambrian</a> strata, which predate land plants — this coal is presumed to have originated from residues of algae.<sup id="cite_ref-Tyler1957_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tyler1957-10">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mancuso1981_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mancuso1981-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ranks">Ranks</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sydney_Mines_Point_Aconi_Seam_038.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Sydney_Mines_Point_Aconi_Seam_038.JPG/220px-Sydney_Mines_Point_Aconi_Seam_038.JPG" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Sydney_Mines_Point_Aconi_Seam_038.JPG/330px-Sydney_Mines_Point_Aconi_Seam_038.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Sydney_Mines_Point_Aconi_Seam_038.JPG/440px-Sydney_Mines_Point_Aconi_Seam_038.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3024" data-file-height="2016" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sydney_Mines_Point_Aconi_Seam_038.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Coastal exposure of the Point Aconi Seam (Nova Scotia)</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Coal_Rank_USGS.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Coal_Rank_USGS.png/220px-Coal_Rank_USGS.png" width="220" height="275" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Coal_Rank_USGS.png/330px-Coal_Rank_USGS.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Coal_Rank_USGS.png/440px-Coal_Rank_USGS.png 2x" data-file-width="2132" data-file-height="2665" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Coal_Rank_USGS.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Coal ranking system used in the United States (US Geological Survey)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>As geological processes apply <a href="/wiki/Pressure" title="Pressure">pressure</a> to dead <a href="/wiki/Biotic_material" title="Biotic material">biotic material</a> over time, under suitable conditions, its <a href="/wiki/Metamorphic_grade" class="mw-redirect" title="Metamorphic grade">metamorphic grade</a> increases successively into:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Peat" title="Peat">Peat</a>, considered to be a precursor of coal, has industrial importance as a fuel in some regions, for example, Ireland and Finland. In its dehydrated form, peat is a highly effective absorbent for fuel and <a href="/wiki/Oil_spill" title="Oil spill">oil spills</a> on land and water. It is also used as a conditioner for soil to make it more able to retain and slowly release water.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lignite" title="Lignite">Lignite</a>, or brown coal, is the lowest rank of coal and used almost exclusively as fuel for electric power generation.
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jet_(lignite)" title="Jet (lignite)">Jet</a>, a compact form of lignite, is sometimes polished and has been used as an ornamental stone since the <a href="/wiki/Upper_Palaeolithic" class="mw-redirect" title="Upper Palaeolithic">Upper Palaeolithic</a>.</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sub-bituminous_coal" title="Sub-bituminous coal">Sub-bituminous coal</a>, whose properties range from those of lignite to those of bituminous coal, is used primarily as fuel for steam-electric power generation and is an important source of light <a href="/wiki/Aromatic_hydrocarbon" title="Aromatic hydrocarbon">aromatic hydrocarbons</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_synthesis" title="Chemical synthesis">chemical synthesis</a> industry.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bituminous_coal" title="Bituminous coal">Bituminous coal</a> is a dense sedimentary rock, usually black, but sometimes dark brown, often with well-defined bands of bright and dull material; it is used primarily as fuel in steam-electric power generation, with substantial quantities used for heat and power applications in manufacturing and to make <a href="/wiki/Coke_(fuel)" title="Coke (fuel)">coke</a>.</li>
<li>"Steam coal" is a grade between bituminous coal and anthracite, once widely used as a fuel for <a href="/wiki/Steam_locomotive" title="Steam locomotive">steam locomotives</a>. In this specialized use, it is sometimes known as "sea coal" in the US.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Small steam coal (dry small steam nuts or DSSN) was used as a fuel for domestic <a href="/wiki/Water_heating" title="Water heating">water heating</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Anthracite" title="Anthracite">Anthracite</a>, the highest rank of coal, is a harder, glossy black coal used primarily for residential and commercial <a href="/wiki/Space_heating" class="mw-redirect" title="Space heating">space heating</a>. It may be divided further into metamorphically altered bituminous coal and "petrified oil", as from the deposits in Pennsylvania.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Graphite" title="Graphite">Graphite</a> is one of the more difficult coals to ignite and is not commonly used as fuel — it is mostly used in pencils, and when powdered, as a <a href="/wiki/Lubricant" title="Lubricant">lubricant</a>.</li>
</ul>
<p>The classification of coal is generally based on the content of volatiles. However, the exact classification varies between countries. According to the German classification, coal is classified as follows:<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>

<p>The middle six grades in the table represent a progressive transition from the English-language sub-bituminous to bituminous coal, while the last class is an approximate equivalent to anthracite, but more inclusive (US anthracite has &lt; 6% volatiles).</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Cannel_coal" title="Cannel coal">Cannel coal</a> (sometimes called "candle coal") is a variety of fine-grained, high-rank coal with significant hydrogen content. It consists primarily of "<a href="/wiki/Exinite" class="mw-redirect" title="Exinite">exinite</a>" <a href="/wiki/Maceral" title="Maceral">macerals</a>, now termed "liptinite".</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hilt.27s_law">Hilt's law</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hilt%27s_law" title="Hilt's law">Hilt's law</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hilt%27s_law" title="Hilt's law">Hilt's law</a> is a geological term that states that, in a small area, the deeper the coal, the higher its rank (grade). The law holds true if the thermal gradient is entirely vertical, but metamorphism may cause lateral changes of rank, irrespective of depth.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_uses_as_fuel">Early uses as fuel</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_coal_mining" title="History of coal mining">History of coal mining</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tiangong_Kaiwu_Coal_Mining.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/27/Tiangong_Kaiwu_Coal_Mining.gif/170px-Tiangong_Kaiwu_Coal_Mining.gif" width="170" height="265" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/2/27/Tiangong_Kaiwu_Coal_Mining.gif 1.5x" data-file-width="212" data-file-height="330" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tiangong_Kaiwu_Coal_Mining.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Chinese coal miners in an illustration of the <i>Tiangong Kaiwu</i> encyclopedia, published in 1637</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The earliest recognized use is from the <a href="/wiki/Shenyang" title="Shenyang">Shenyang</a> area of China 4000 BC where <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> inhabitants had begun carving ornaments from black lignite.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Coal from the Fushun mine in northeastern China was used to smelt copper as early as 1000 BCE.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Marco_Polo" title="Marco Polo">Marco Polo</a>, the Italian who traveled to China in the 13th century, described coal as "black stones&#160;... which burn like logs", and said coal was so plentiful, people could take three hot baths a week.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> In Europe, the earliest reference to the use of coal as fuel is from the geological treatise <i>On stones</i> (Lap. 16) by the Greek scientist <a href="/wiki/Theophrastus" title="Theophrastus">Theophrastus</a> (<i>circa</i> 371–287 BC):<sup id="cite_ref-Mattusch_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mattusch-20">[20]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Among the materials that are dug because they are useful, those known as <i>anthrakes</i> [coals] are made of earth, and, once set on fire, they burn like charcoal. They are found in Liguria&#160;... and in Elis as one approaches Olympia by the mountain road; and they are used by those who work in metals.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Theophrastus, On Stones (16) <i>translation</i></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Outcrop" title="Outcrop">Outcrop</a> coal was used in Britain during the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> (3000–2000 BC), where it has been detected as forming part of the composition of <a href="/wiki/Funeral" title="Funeral">funeral</a> <a href="/wiki/Pyre" title="Pyre">pyres</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Britannicacoal_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britannicacoal-22">[22]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Roman_Britain" title="Roman Britain">Roman Britain</a>, with the exception of two modern fields, "the <a href="/wiki/Mining_in_Roman_Britain" title="Mining in Roman Britain">Romans</a> were exploiting coals in all the major coalfields in <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a> by the end of the second century AD".<sup id="cite_ref-Smith_1997.2C_322.E2.80.93324_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith_1997.2C_322.E2.80.93324-24">[24]</a></sup> Evidence of trade in coal (dated to about AD 200) has been found at the <a href="/wiki/Heronbridge_Roman_Site" title="Heronbridge Roman Site">Roman settlement at Heronbridge</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Chester" title="Chester">Chester</a>, and in the <a href="/wiki/The_Fens" title="The Fens">Fenlands</a> of <a href="/wiki/East_Anglia" title="East Anglia">East Anglia</a>, where coal from the <a href="/wiki/English_Midlands" class="mw-redirect" title="English Midlands">Midlands</a> was transported via the <a href="/wiki/Car_Dyke" title="Car Dyke">Car Dyke</a> for use in drying grain.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Coal cinders have been found in the hearths of <a href="/wiki/Roman_villa" title="Roman villa">villas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roman_forts" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman forts">Roman forts</a>, particularly in <a href="/wiki/Northumberland" title="Northumberland">Northumberland</a>, dated to around AD 400. In the west of England, contemporary writers described the wonder of a permanent brazier of coal on the altar of <a href="/wiki/Minerva" title="Minerva">Minerva</a> at <a href="/wiki/Aquae_Sulis" title="Aquae Sulis">Aquae Sulis</a> (modern day <a href="/wiki/Bath,_Somerset" title="Bath, Somerset">Bath</a>), although in fact easily accessible surface coal from what became the <a href="/wiki/Somerset_coalfield" class="mw-redirect" title="Somerset coalfield">Somerset coalfield</a> was in common use in quite lowly dwellings locally.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> Evidence of coal's use for iron-working in the city during the Roman period has been found.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Eschweiler" title="Eschweiler">Eschweiler</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rhineland" title="Rhineland">Rhineland</a>, deposits of <a href="/wiki/Bituminous_coal" title="Bituminous coal">bituminous coal</a> were used by the Romans for the smelting of <a href="/wiki/Iron_ore" title="Iron ore">iron ore</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Smith_1997.2C_322.E2.80.93324_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith_1997.2C_322.E2.80.93324-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Men_of_the_Mine-_Life_at_the_Coal_Face,_Britain,_1942_D8263.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Men_of_the_Mine-_Life_at_the_Coal_Face%2C_Britain%2C_1942_D8263.jpg/220px-Men_of_the_Mine-_Life_at_the_Coal_Face%2C_Britain%2C_1942_D8263.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Men_of_the_Mine-_Life_at_the_Coal_Face%2C_Britain%2C_1942_D8263.jpg/330px-Men_of_the_Mine-_Life_at_the_Coal_Face%2C_Britain%2C_1942_D8263.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Men_of_the_Mine-_Life_at_the_Coal_Face%2C_Britain%2C_1942_D8263.jpg/440px-Men_of_the_Mine-_Life_at_the_Coal_Face%2C_Britain%2C_1942_D8263.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="533" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Men_of_the_Mine-_Life_at_the_Coal_Face,_Britain,_1942_D8263.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Coal miner in Britain, 1942</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>No evidence exists of the product being of great importance in Britain before the <a href="/wiki/High_Middle_Ages" title="High Middle Ages">High Middle Ages</a>, after about AD 1000.<sup id="cite_ref-TCC_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TCC-28">[28]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mineral" title="Mineral">Mineral</a> coal came to be referred to as "seacoal" in the 13th century; the wharf where the material arrived in London was known as Seacoal Lane, so identified in a charter of <a href="/wiki/Henry_III_of_England" title="Henry III of England">King Henry III</a> granted in 1253.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Initially, the name was given because much coal was found on the shore, having fallen from the exposed <a href="/wiki/Coal_seam" class="mw-redirect" title="Coal seam">coal seams</a> on cliffs above or washed out of underwater coal outcrops,<sup id="cite_ref-TCC_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TCC-28">[28]</a></sup> but by the time of <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" title="Henry VIII of England">Henry VIII</a>, it was understood to derive from the way it was carried to London by sea.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> In 1257–59, coal from <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_upon_Tyne" title="Newcastle upon Tyne">Newcastle upon Tyne</a> was shipped to London for the smiths and lime-burners building <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-TCC_28-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TCC-28">[28]</a></sup> Seacoal Lane and Newcastle Lane, where coal was unloaded at wharves along the <a href="/wiki/River_Fleet" title="River Fleet">River Fleet</a>, are still in existence.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> (See <a href="/wiki/Coal#Industrial_processes" title="Coal">Industrial processes</a> below for modern uses of the term.)</p>
<p>These easily accessible sources had largely become exhausted (or could not meet the growing demand) by the 13th century, when underground extraction by <a href="/wiki/Shaft_mining" title="Shaft mining">shaft mining</a> or <a href="/wiki/Adit" title="Adit">adits</a> was developed.<sup id="cite_ref-Britannicacoal_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britannicacoal-22">[22]</a></sup> The alternative name was "pitcoal", because it came from mines. The development of the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a> led to the large-scale use of coal, as the <a href="/wiki/Steam_engine" title="Steam engine">steam engine</a> took over from the <a href="/wiki/Water_wheel" title="Water wheel">water wheel</a>. In 1700, five-sixths of the world's coal was mined in Britain. Britain would have run out of suitable sites for watermills by the 1830s if coal had not been available as a source of energy.<sup id="cite_ref-Wrigley_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wrigley-32">[32]</a></sup> In 1947, there were some 750,000 miners in Britain,<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_News_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_News-33">[33]</a></sup> but by 2004, this had shrunk to some 5,000 miners working in around 20 collieries.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_Inside_Out_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_Inside_Out-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Uses_today">Uses today</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Castle_Gate_Power_Plant,_Utah_2007.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Castle_Gate_Power_Plant%2C_Utah_2007.jpg/220px-Castle_Gate_Power_Plant%2C_Utah_2007.jpg" width="220" height="138" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Castle_Gate_Power_Plant%2C_Utah_2007.jpg/330px-Castle_Gate_Power_Plant%2C_Utah_2007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Castle_Gate_Power_Plant%2C_Utah_2007.jpg/440px-Castle_Gate_Power_Plant%2C_Utah_2007.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2293" data-file-height="1439" /></a>
