<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coal_as_fuel">Coal as fuel</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Electricity_generation" title="Electricity generation">Electricity generation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Clean_coal_technology" title="Clean coal technology">Clean coal technology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coal_electricity" class="mw-redirect" title="Coal electricity">Coal electricity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">Global warming</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_coal_power_stations" title="List of coal power stations">List of coal power stations</a></div>
<p>Coal is primarily used as a <a href="/wiki/Solid_fuel" title="Solid fuel">solid fuel</a> to produce electricity and heat through combustion. World coal consumption was about 7.25 billion <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a> in 2010<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> (7.99 billion <a href="/wiki/Short_ton" title="Short ton">short tons</a>) and is expected to increase 48% to 9.05 billion tonnes (9.98 billion short tons) by 2030.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Efforts around the world to reduce the use of coal has led some regions to <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel_phase-out#Switch_to_natural_gas" title="Fossil fuel phase-out">switch to natural gas</a>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Coal_power_in_China" class="mw-redirect" title="Coal power in China">China</a> produced 3.47 billion tonnes (3.83 billion short tons) in 2011. <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> produced about 578 million tonnes (637.1 million short tons) in 2011. 68.7% of China's electricity comes from coal. The US consumed about 13% of the world total in 2010, i.e. 951 million tonnes (1.05 billion short tons), using 93% of it for generation of electricity.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> 46% of total power generated in the US was using coal.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> The United States <a href="/wiki/Energy_Information_Administration" title="Energy Information Administration">Energy Information Administration</a> estimates coal <a href="/wiki/Mineral_resource_classification" title="Mineral resource classification">reserves</a> at <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7011948000000000000♠</span>948<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>9</sup></span> <a href="/wiki/Short_ton" title="Short ton">short tons</a> (860 <a href="/wiki/Gigatonne" class="mw-redirect" title="Gigatonne">Gt</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-EIAreserves_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EIAreserves-39">[39]</a></sup> One estimate for <a href="/wiki/Mineral_resource_classification" title="Mineral resource classification">resources</a> is 18,000 Gt.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>When coal is used for <a href="/wiki/Electricity_generation" title="Electricity generation">electricity generation</a>, it is usually pulverized and then burned in a <a href="/wiki/Furnace" title="Furnace">furnace</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Boiler" title="Boiler">boiler</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-World_Coal_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-World_Coal-41">[41]</a></sup> The furnace heat converts boiler water to <a href="/wiki/Steam" title="Steam">steam</a>, which is then used to spin <a href="/wiki/Turbine" title="Turbine">turbines</a> which turn <a href="/wiki/Electrical_generator" class="mw-redirect" title="Electrical generator">generators</a> and create electricity.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_efficiency" class="mw-redirect" title="Thermodynamic efficiency">thermodynamic efficiency</a> of this process has been improved over time; some older coal-fired power stations have thermal efficiencies in the vicinity of 25%<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> whereas the newest <a href="/wiki/Supercritical_steam_generator" title="Supercritical steam generator">supercritical</a> and "ultra-supercritical" steam cycle turbines, operating at temperatures over 600&#160;°C and pressures over 27 MPa (over 3900 psi), can achieve thermal efficiencies in excess of 45% (LHV basis) using anthracite fuel,<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> or around 43% (LHV basis) even when using lower-grade lignite fuel.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> Further thermal efficiency improvements are also achievable by improved pre-drying (especially relevant with high-moisture fuel such as lignite or biomass) and cooling technologies.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>An alternative approach of using coal for electricity generation with improved efficiency is the <a href="/wiki/Integrated_gasification_combined_cycle" title="Integrated gasification combined cycle">integrated gasification combined cycle</a> (IGCC) power plant. Instead of pulverizing the coal and burning it directly as fuel in the steam-generating boiler, the coal is gasified (see <a href="/wiki/Coal_gasification" title="Coal gasification">coal gasification</a>) to create <a href="/wiki/Syngas" title="Syngas">syngas</a>, which is burned in a <a href="/wiki/Gas_turbine" title="Gas turbine">gas turbine</a> to produce electricity (just like natural gas is burned in a turbine). Hot exhaust gases from the turbine are used to raise steam in a <a href="/wiki/Heat_recovery_steam_generator" title="Heat recovery steam generator">heat recovery steam generator</a> which powers a supplemental <a href="/wiki/Steam_turbine" title="Steam turbine">steam turbine</a>. Thermal efficiencies of current IGCC power plants range from 39% to 42%<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> (HHV basis) or ≈42–45% (LHV basis) for bituminous coal and assuming utilization of mainstream gasification technologies (Shell, GE Gasifier, CB&amp;I). IGCC power plants outperform conventional pulverized coal-fueled plants in terms of pollutant emissions, and allow for relatively easy carbon capture.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>At least 40% of the world's electricity comes from coal,<sup id="cite_ref-World_Coal_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-World_Coal-41">[41]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> and in 2012, about one-third of the United States' electricity came from coal, down from approximately 49% in 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-NYTKentucky_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTKentucky-50">[50]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> As of 2012 in the United States, use of coal to generate electricity was declining, as plentiful supplies of natural gas obtained by <a href="/wiki/Hydraulic_fracturing" title="Hydraulic fracturing">hydraulic fracturing</a> of tight shale formations became available at low prices.<sup id="cite_ref-NYTKentucky_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTKentucky-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Denmark, a net electric efficiency of &gt; 47% has been obtained at the coal-fired Nordjyllandsværket CHP Plant and an overall plant efficiency of up to 91% with cogeneration of electricity and district heating.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> The multifuel-fired Avedøreværket CHP Plant just outside Copenhagen can achieve a net electric efficiency as high as 49%. The overall plant efficiency with cogeneration of electricity and district heating can reach as much as 94%.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>An alternative form of coal combustion is as <a href="/wiki/Coal-water_slurry_fuel" title="Coal-water slurry fuel">coal-water slurry fuel</a> (CWS), which was developed in the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>. Other ways to use coal are <a href="/wiki/Cogeneration" title="Cogeneration">combined heat and power cogeneration</a> and <a href="/wiki/Magnetohydrodynamic_generator" title="Magnetohydrodynamic generator">an MHD topping cycle</a>.</p>
<p>The total known deposits recoverable by current technologies, including highly polluting, low-energy content types of coal (i.e., lignite, bituminous), is sufficient for many years.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="This term requires quantification. (May 2012)">quantify</span></a></i>]</sup> Consumption is increasing and <a href="/wiki/Peak_coal" title="Peak coal">maximal production could be reached</a> within decades (see <a href="/wiki/Coal#World_coal_reserves" title="Coal">world coal reserves</a>, below). On the other hand, much may have to be left in the ground to avoid climate change.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coking_coal_and_use_of_coke">Coking coal and use of coke</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Coke_(fuel)" title="Coke (fuel)">Coke (fuel)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Coke_Ovens_Abercwmboi.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Coke_Ovens_Abercwmboi.jpg/220px-Coke_Ovens_Abercwmboi.jpg" width="220" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Coke_Ovens_Abercwmboi.jpg/330px-Coke_Ovens_Abercwmboi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Coke_Ovens_Abercwmboi.jpg/440px-Coke_Ovens_Abercwmboi.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3039" data-file-height="2136" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Coke_Ovens_Abercwmboi.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Coke oven at a <a href="/wiki/Smokeless_fuel" title="Smokeless fuel">smokeless fuel</a> plant in <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a>, United Kingdom</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Coke is a solid carbonaceous residue derived from low-ash, low-sulfur bituminous coal (metallurgical coal), from which the volatile constituents are driven off by baking in an oven without oxygen at temperatures as high as 1,000&#160;°C (1,832&#160;°F), so the fixed carbon and residual ash are fused together. Metallurgical coke is used as a fuel and as a <a href="/wiki/Reducing_agent" title="Reducing agent">reducing agent</a> in smelting <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> ore in a <a href="/wiki/Blast_furnace" title="Blast furnace">blast furnace</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> The result is <a href="/wiki/Pig_iron" title="Pig iron">pig iron</a>, and is too rich in dissolved carbon, so it must be treated further to make <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a>. The coking coal should be low in <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a>, so they do not migrate to the metal. Based on the ash percentage, the coking coal can be divided into various grades. These grades are:</p>
<ul>
<li>Steel Grade I (Ash content not exceeding 15%)</li>
<li>Steel Grade II (Exceeding 15% but not exceeding 18%)</li>
<li>Washery Grade I (Exceeding 18% but not exceeding 21%)</li>
<li>Washery Grade II (Exceeding 21% but not exceeding 24%)</li>
<li>Washery Grade III (Exceeding 24% but not exceeding 28%)</li>
<li>Washery Grade IV (Exceeding 28% but not exceeding 35%)<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>The coke must be <a href="/wiki/Coke_strength_after_reaction" title="Coke strength after reaction">strong enough</a> to resist the weight of overburden in the blast furnace, which is why coking coal is so important in making steel using the conventional route. However, the alternative route is <a href="/wiki/Direct_reduced_iron" title="Direct reduced iron">direct reduced iron</a>, where any carbonaceous fuel can be used to make sponge or pelletised iron. Coke from coal is grey, hard, and porous and has a heating value of 24.8 million Btu/ton (29.6 MJ/kg). Some cokemaking processes produce valuable byproducts, including <a href="/wiki/Coal_tar" title="Coal tar">coal tar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>, light oils, and <a href="/wiki/Coal_gas" title="Coal gas">coal gas</a>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Petroleum_coke" title="Petroleum coke">Petroleum coke</a> is the solid residue obtained in <a href="/wiki/Oil_refining" class="mw-redirect" title="Oil refining">oil refining</a>, which resembles coke, but contains too many impurities to be useful in metallurgical applications.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gasification">Gasification</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Coal_gasification" title="Coal gasification">Coal gasification</a> and <a href="/wiki/Underground_coal_gasification" title="Underground coal gasification">Underground coal gasification</a></div>
<p>Coal gasification can be used to produce <a href="/wiki/Syngas" title="Syngas">syngas</a>, a mixture of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a> (CO) and hydrogen (H<sub>2</sub>) gas. Often syngas is used to fire gas turbines to produce electricity, but the versatility of syngas also allows it to be converted into transportation fuels, such as gasoline and diesel, through the <a href="/wiki/Fischer-Tropsch_process" class="mw-redirect" title="Fischer-Tropsch process">Fischer-Tropsch process</a>; alternatively, syngas can be converted into <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a>, which can be blended into fuel directly or converted to gasoline via the methanol to gasoline process.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Gasification combined with Fischer-Tropsch technology is currently used by the <a href="/wiki/Sasol" title="Sasol">Sasol</a> chemical company of <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a> to make motor vehicle fuels from coal and natural gas. Alternatively, the hydrogen obtained from gasification can be used for various purposes, such as powering a <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_economy" title="Hydrogen economy">hydrogen economy</a>, making ammonia, or upgrading fossil fuels.</p>
<p>During gasification, the coal is mixed with <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Steam" title="Steam">steam</a> while also being heated and pressurized. During the reaction, oxygen and water molecules <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">oxidize</a> the coal into carbon monoxide (CO), while also releasing <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> gas (H<sub>2</sub>). This process has been conducted in both underground coal mines and in the production of <a href="/wiki/Town_gas" class="mw-redirect" title="Town gas">town gas</a> which was piped to customers to burn for illumination, heating, and cooking.</p>
<dl>
<dd>C (<i>as Coal</i>) + O<sub>2</sub> + H<sub>2</sub>O → H<sub>2</sub> + CO</dd>
</dl>
<p>If the refiner wants to produce gasoline, the syngas is collected at this state and routed into a Fischer-Tropsch reaction. If hydrogen is the desired end-product, however, the syngas is fed into the <a href="/wiki/Water_gas_shift_reaction" class="mw-redirect" title="Water gas shift reaction">water gas shift reaction</a>, where more hydrogen is liberated.</p>
<dl>
<dd>CO + H<sub>2</sub>O → CO<sub>2</sub> + H<sub>2</sub></dd>
</dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Liquefaction">Liquefaction</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Coal_liquefaction" title="Coal liquefaction">Coal liquefaction</a></div>
<p>Coal can also be converted into <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_fuel" title="Synthetic fuel">synthetic fuels</a> equivalent to <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">gasoline</a> or <a href="/wiki/Diesel_fuel" title="Diesel fuel">diesel</a> by several different direct processes (which do not intrinsically require gasification or indirect conversion).<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> In the direct liquefaction processes, the coal is either <a href="/wiki/Hydrogenation" title="Hydrogenation">hydrogenated</a> or <a href="/wiki/Carbonization" title="Carbonization">carbonized</a>. Hydrogenation processes are the <a href="/wiki/Bergius_process" title="Bergius process">Bergius process</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> the SRC-I and SRC-II (Solvent Refined Coal) processes, the NUS Corporation hydrogenation process<sup id="cite_ref-handbook2_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-handbook2-61">[61]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> and several other single-stage and two-stage processes.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> In the process of low-temperature <a href="/wiki/Carbonization" title="Carbonization">carbonization</a>, coal is coked at temperatures between 360 and 750&#160;°C (680 and 1,380&#160;°F). These temperatures optimize the production of coal tars richer in lighter hydrocarbons than normal coal tar. The coal tar is then further processed into fuels. An overview of coal liquefaction and its future potential is available.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>Coal liquefaction methods involve carbon dioxide (CO<sub>2</sub>) emissions in the conversion process. If coal liquefaction is done without employing either <a href="/wiki/Carbon_capture_and_storage" title="Carbon capture and storage">carbon capture and storage</a> (CCS) technologies or biomass blending, the result is lifecycle greenhouse gas footprints that are generally greater than those released in the extraction and refinement of liquid fuel production from <a href="/wiki/Crude_oil" class="mw-redirect" title="Crude oil">crude oil</a>. If CCS technologies are employed, reductions of 5–12% can be achieved in <a href="/wiki/Coal_to_liquids" class="mw-redirect" title="Coal to liquids">Coal to Liquid (CTL)</a> plants and up to a 75% reduction is achievable when co-gasifying coal with commercially demonstrated levels of biomass (30% biomass by weight) in coal/biomass-to-liquids plants.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> For future <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_fuel" title="Synthetic fuel">synthetic fuel</a> projects, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_sequestration" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbon dioxide sequestration">carbon dioxide sequestration</a> is proposed to avoid releasing CO<sub>2</sub> into the atmosphere. Sequestration adds to the cost of production.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Refined_coal">Refined coal</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Refined_coal" title="Refined coal">Refined coal</a></div>
<p>Refined coal is the product of a coal-upgrading technology that removes moisture and certain pollutants from lower-rank coals such as sub-bituminous and lignite (brown) coals. It is one form of several precombustion treatments and processes for coal that alter coal's characteristics before it is burned. The goals of precombustion coal technologies are to increase efficiency and reduce emissions when the coal is burned. Depending on the situation, precombustion technology can be used in place of or as a supplement to postcombustion technologies to control emissions from coal-fueled boilers.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Industrial_processes">Industrial processes <span id="Industrial_processes"></span><span id="Sea_coal"></span></span></h3>
<p>Finely ground bituminous coal, known in this application as sea coal, is a constituent of <a href="/wiki/Foundry_sand" class="mw-redirect" title="Foundry sand">foundry sand</a>. While the molten metal is in the <a href="/wiki/Molding_(process)" title="Molding (process)">mould</a>, the coal burns slowly, releasing <a href="/wiki/Reducing_agent" title="Reducing agent">reducing gases</a> at pressure, and so preventing the metal from penetrating the pores of the sand. It is also contained in 'mould wash', a paste or liquid with the same function applied to the mould before casting.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Sea coal can be mixed with the clay lining (the "bod") used for the bottom of a <a href="/wiki/Cupola_furnace" title="Cupola furnace">cupola furnace</a>. When heated, the coal decomposes and the bod becomes slightly friable, easing the process of breaking open holes for tapping the molten metal.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Production_of_chemicals">Production of chemicals</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Coal_to_chemicals_routes_diagram.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/9e/Coal_to_chemicals_routes_diagram.jpg/220px-Coal_to_chemicals_routes_diagram.jpg" width="220" height="131" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/9e/Coal_to_chemicals_routes_diagram.jpg/330px-Coal_to_chemicals_routes_diagram.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/9e/Coal_to_chemicals_routes_diagram.jpg/440px-Coal_to_chemicals_routes_diagram.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="478" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Coal_to_chemicals_routes_diagram.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Production of chemicals from coal</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Coal is an important feedstock in production of a wide range of chemical fertilizers and other chemical products.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> The main route to these products is <a href="/wiki/Coal_gasification" title="Coal gasification">coal gasification</a> to produce <a href="/wiki/Syngas" title="Syngas">syngas</a>. Primary chemicals that are produced directly from the syngas include <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a>, which are the chemical building blocks from which a whole spectrum of derivative chemicals are manufactured, including <a href="/wiki/Olefins" class="mw-redirect" title="Olefins">olefins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acetic_acid" title="Acetic acid">acetic acid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Formaldehyde" title="Formaldehyde">formaldehyde</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Urea" title="Urea">urea</a> and others. The versatility of <a href="/wiki/Syngas" title="Syngas">syngas</a> as a precursor to primary chemicals and high-value derivative products provides the option of using relatively inexpensive coal to produce a wide range of valuable commodities.</p>
<p>Historically, production of chemicals from coal has been used since the 1950s and has become established in the market. According to the 2010 Worldwide Gasification Database,<sup id="cite_ref-DOEGasificationDatabase2010_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DOEGasificationDatabase2010-69">[69]</a></sup> a survey of current and planned gasifiers, from 2004 to 2007 chemical production increased its gasification product share from 37% to 45%. From 2008 to 2010, 22% of new gasifier additions were to be for chemical production.</p>
<p>Because the slate of chemical products that can be made via coal gasification can in general also use feedstocks derived from <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a>, the chemical industry tends to use whatever feedstocks are most cost-effective. Therefore, interest in using coal tends to increase for higher oil and natural gas prices and during periods of high global economic growth that may strain oil and gas production. Also, production of chemicals from coal is of much higher interest in countries like South Africa, China, India and the United States where there are abundant coal resources. The abundance of coal combined with lack of natural gas resources in China is strong inducement for the coal to chemicals industry pursued there. In the United States, the best example of the industry is Eastman Chemical Company which has been successfully operating a coal-to-chemicals plant at its Kingsport, Tennessee, site since 1983. Similarly, Sasol has built and operated coal-to-chemicals facilities in South Africa.</p>
<p>Coal to chemical processes do require substantial quantities of water. As of 2013 much of the coal to chemical production was in the People's Republic of China<sup id="cite_ref-OILDRUM9371_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OILDRUM9371-70">[70]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ICIS022712_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ICIS022712-71">[71]</a></sup> where environmental regulation and <a href="/wiki/Water_resource_management" title="Water resource management">water management</a><sup id="cite_ref-IHT02513_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHT02513-72">[72]</a></sup> was weak.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT020213_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT020213-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Coal_industry">Coal industry</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main pages: <a href="/wiki/Category:Coal_companies" title="Category:Coal companies">Category:Coal companies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coal_mining" title="Coal mining">Coal mining</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coal_industry_in_China" class="mw-redirect" title="Coal industry in China">Coal industry in China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coal_industry_in_Pakistan" class="mw-redirect" title="Coal industry in Pakistan">Coal industry in Pakistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coal_industry_in_India" class="mw-redirect" title="Coal industry in India">Coal industry in India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coal_industry_in_New_Zealand" class="mw-redirect" title="Coal industry in New Zealand">Coal industry in New Zealand</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Coal_companies_of_Australia" title="Coal companies of Australia">Coal companies of Australia</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Coal_Industry_Nationalisation_Act_1946" title="Coal Industry Nationalisation Act 1946">Coal Industry Nationalisation Act 1946</a> and <a href="/wiki/Coal_Industry_Commission_Act_1919" title="Coal Industry Commission Act 1919">Coal Industry Commission Act 1919</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coal_as_a_traded_commodity">Coal as a traded commodity</span></h3>
<p>In North America, Central <a href="/wiki/Appalachia" title="Appalachia">Appalachian</a> coal <a href="/wiki/Futures_contract" title="Futures contract">futures contracts</a> are currently traded on the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Mercantile_Exchange" title="New York Mercantile Exchange">New York Mercantile Exchange</a> (trading symbol <i>QL</i>). The trading unit is 1,550 short tons (1,410&#160;t) per contract, and is quoted in U.S. dollars and cents per ton. Since coal is the principal fuel for generating electricity in the United States, coal futures contracts provide coal producers and the <a href="/wiki/Electric_power_industry" title="Electric power industry">electric power industry</a> an important tool for <a href="/wiki/Hedge_(finance)" title="Hedge (finance)">hedging</a> and <a href="/wiki/Risk_management" title="Risk management">risk management</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>In addition to the NYMEX contract, the <a href="/wiki/Intercontinental_Exchange" title="Intercontinental Exchange">Intercontinental Exchange</a> (ICE) has European (Rotterdam) and South African (Richards Bay) coal futures available for trading. The trading unit for these contracts is 5,000 tonnes (5,500 short tons), and are also quoted in U.S. dollars and cents per ton.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>The price of coal increased from around $30.00 per <a href="/wiki/Short_ton" title="Short ton">short ton</a> in 2000 to around $150.00 per short ton as of September 2008. As of October 2008, the price per short ton had declined to $111.50. Prices further declined to $71.25 as of October 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> In early 2015, it was trading near $56/ton.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_and_health_effects">Environmental and health effects</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_the_coal_industry" title="Environmental impact of the coal industry">Environmental impact of the coal industry</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aerial_view_of_ash_slide_site_Dec_23_2008_TVA.gov_123002.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Aerial_view_of_ash_slide_site_Dec_23_2008_TVA.gov_123002.jpg/220px-Aerial_view_of_ash_slide_site_Dec_23_2008_TVA.gov_123002.jpg" width="220" height="272" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Aerial_view_of_ash_slide_site_Dec_23_2008_TVA.gov_123002.jpg/330px-Aerial_view_of_ash_slide_site_Dec_23_2008_TVA.gov_123002.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Aerial_view_of_ash_slide_site_Dec_23_2008_TVA.gov_123002.jpg/440px-Aerial_view_of_ash_slide_site_Dec_23_2008_TVA.gov_123002.jpg 2x" data-file-width="828" data-file-height="1024" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aerial_view_of_ash_slide_site_Dec_23_2008_TVA.gov_123002.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Aerial photograph of <a href="/wiki/Kingston_Fossil_Plant" title="Kingston Fossil Plant">Kingston Fossil Plant</a> coal fly ash <a href="/wiki/Kingston_Fossil_Plant_coal_fly_ash_slurry_spill" title="Kingston Fossil Plant coal fly ash slurry spill">slurry spill</a> site taken the day after the event</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Health_effects">Health effects</span></h3>
<p>The use of coal as fuel causes adverse health impacts and deaths.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>The deadly <a href="/wiki/Pea_soup_fog#London" title="Pea soup fog">London smog</a> was caused primarily by the heavy use of coal. In the United States coal-fired power plants were estimated in 2004 to cause nearly 24,000 premature deaths every year, including 2,800 from <a href="/wiki/Lung_cancer" title="Lung cancer">lung cancer</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> Annual health costs in Europe from use of coal to generate electricity are €42.8 billion, or $55 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> Yet the disease and mortality burden of coal use today falls most heavily upon China.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p>Breathing in <a href="/wiki/Coal_dust" title="Coal dust">coal dust</a> causes <a href="/wiki/Coalworker%27s_pneumoconiosis" title="Coalworker's pneumoconiosis">coalworker's pneumoconiosis</a> which is known colloquially as "black lung", so-called because the coal dust literally turns the lungs black from their usual pink color.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> In the United States alone, it is estimated that 1,500 former employees of the coal industry die every year from the effects of breathing in coal mine dust.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<p>Around 10% of coal is ash,<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Coal_ash" class="mw-redirect" title="Coal ash">Coal ash</a> is hazardous and toxic to human beings and other living things.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> Coal ash contains the radioactive elements <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thorium" title="Thorium">thorium</a>. Coal ash and other solid combustion byproducts are stored locally and escape in various ways that expose those living near coal plants to radiation and environmental toxics.<sup id="cite_ref-sa-coal-ash-more-rdactiv_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sa-coal-ash-more-rdactiv-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>Huge amounts of coal ash and other waste is produced annually. In 2013, the US alone consumed on the order of 983 million short tonnes of coal per year.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> Use of coal on this scale generates hundreds of millions of tons of ash and other waste products every year. These include <a href="/wiki/Fly_ash" title="Fly ash">fly ash</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bottom_ash" title="Bottom ash">bottom ash</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Flue-gas_desulfurization" title="Flue-gas desulfurization">flue-gas desulfurization</a> sludge, that contain <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thorium" title="Thorium">thorium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arsenic" title="Arsenic">arsenic</a>, and other <a href="/wiki/Heavy_metals" title="Heavy metals">heavy metals</a>, along with non-metals such as <a href="/wiki/Selenium" title="Selenium">selenium</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/American_Lung_Association" title="American Lung Association">American Lung Association</a>, the <a href="/wiki/American_Nurses%27_Association" class="mw-redirect" title="American Nurses' Association">American Nurses' Association</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Physicians_for_Social_Responsibility" title="Physicians for Social Responsibility">Physicians for Social Responsibility</a> released a report in 2009 which details in depth the detrimental impact of the coal industry on human health, including workers in the mines and individuals living in communities near plants burning coal as a power source. This report provides medical information regarding damage to the lungs, heart, and nervous system of Americans caused by the burning of coal as fuel. It details how the air pollution caused by the plume of coal smokestack emissions is a cause of asthma, strokes, reduced intelligence, artery blockages, heart attacks, congestive heart failure, cardiac arrhythmias, mercury poisoning, arterial occlusion, and lung cancer.<sup id="cite_ref-psr_coal-damages-health_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psr_coal-damages-health-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>More recently, the Chicago School of Public Health released a largely similar report, echoing many of the same findings.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<p>Though coal burning has increasingly been supplanted by less-toxic natural gas use in recent years, a 2010 study by the Clean Air Task Force still estimated that "air pollution from coal-fired power plants accounts for more than 13,000 premature deaths, 20,000 heart attacks, and 1.6 million lost workdays in the U.S. each year." The total monetary cost of these health impacts is over $100 billion annually.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>The WHO classifies coal as a "dirty fuel" and encourages the movement away from such fuels towards cleaner alternatives.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_effects">Environmental effects</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Coal_mining" title="Coal mining">Coal mining</a> and coal fueling of <a href="/wiki/Power_station" title="Power station">power station</a> and industrial processes can cause major environmental damage.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>Water systems are affected by coal mining.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> For example, mining affects <a href="/wiki/Groundwater" title="Groundwater">groundwater</a> and <a href="/wiki/Water_table" title="Water table">water table</a> levels and acidity. Spills of fly ash, such as the <a href="/wiki/Kingston_Fossil_Plant_coal_fly_ash_slurry_spill" title="Kingston Fossil Plant coal fly ash slurry spill">Kingston Fossil Plant coal fly ash slurry spill</a>, can also contaminate land and waterways, and destroy homes. Power stations that burn coal also consume large quantities of water. This can affect the flows of rivers, and has consequential impacts on other land uses.</p>
<p>One of the earliest known impacts of coal on the water cycle was <a href="/wiki/Acid_rain" title="Acid rain">acid rain</a>. Approximately 75 Tg/S per year of <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide">sulfur dioxide</a> (SO<sub>2</sub>) is released from burning coal. After release, the sulfur dioxide is oxidized to gaseous H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>2</sub> which scatters solar radiation, hence its increase in the atmosphere exerts a cooling effect on climate. This beneficially masks some of the warming caused by increased greenhouse gases. However, the sulfur is precipitated out of the atmosphere as acid rain in a matter of weeks,<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> whereas carbon dioxide remains in the atmosphere for hundreds of years. Release of SO<sub>2</sub> also contributes to the widespread acidification of ecosystems.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p>Disused coal mines can also cause issues. Subsidence can occur above tunnels, causing damage to infrastructure or cropland. Coal mining can also cause long lasting fires, and it has been estimated that thousands of <a href="/wiki/Coal_seam_fire" title="Coal seam fire">coal seam fires</a> are burning at any given time.<sup id="cite_ref-Deep_underground_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Deep_underground-97">[97]</a></sup> For example, there is a <a href="/wiki/Coal_seam_fire#Germany" title="Coal seam fire">coal seam fire in Germany</a> that has been burning since 1668, and is still burning in the 21st century.<sup id="cite_ref-mineralienatlas_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mineralienatlas-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some environmental impacts are modest, such as dust nuisance. However, perhaps the largest and most long term effect of coal use is the release of carbon dioxide, a <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a> that causes <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a> and <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a>, according to the <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change" title="Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change">IPCC</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="United States Environmental Protection Agency">EPA</a>. Coal is the largest contributor to the human-made increase of CO<sub>2</sub> in the atmosphere.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<p>The production of coke from coal produces <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>, coal tar, and gaseous compounds as by-products which if discharged to land, air or waterways can act as environmental pollutants.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Whyalla_steelworks" class="mw-redirect" title="Whyalla steelworks">Whyalla steelworks</a> is one example of a coke producing facility where liquid ammonia is discharged to the marine environment.</p>
<p>In 1999, world gross <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_emissions" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbon dioxide emissions">carbon dioxide emissions</a> from coal usage were 8,666 million tonnes of carbon dioxide.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> In 2011, world gross emissions from coal usage were 14,416 million tonnes.<sup id="cite_ref-US.EIA_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-US.EIA-102">[102]</a></sup> For every megawatt-hour generated, coal-fired electric power generation emits around 2,000 pounds of carbon dioxide, which is almost double the approximately 1100 pounds of carbon dioxide released by a <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a>-fired electric plant.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> Because of this higher carbon efficiency of natural gas generation, as the market in the United States has changed to reduce coal and increase natural gas generation, carbon dioxide emissions may have fallen.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> Those measured in the first quarter of 2012 were the lowest of any recorded for the first quarter of any year since 1992.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> In 2013, the head of the UN climate agency advised that most of the world's coal reserves should be left in the ground to avoid catastrophic global warming.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Clean_coal_technology">Clean coal technology</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Clean_coal_technology" title="Clean coal technology">Clean coal technology</a></div>
<p>"Clean" coal technology is a collection of technologies being developed to mitigate the environmental impact of coal energy generation.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> Those technologies are being developed to remove or reduce pollutant emissions to the atmosphere. Some of the techniques that would be used to accomplish this include chemically washing <a href="/wiki/Mineral" title="Mineral">minerals</a> and impurities from the coal, <a href="/wiki/Gasification" title="Gasification">gasification</a> (see also <a href="/wiki/Integrated_gasification_combined_cycle" title="Integrated gasification combined cycle">IGCC</a>), improved technology for treating <a href="/wiki/Flue" title="Flue">flue</a> gases to remove pollutants to increasingly stringent levels and at higher efficiency, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_capture_and_storage" title="Carbon capture and storage">carbon capture and storage</a> technologies to capture the carbon dioxide from the <a href="/wiki/Flue_gas" title="Flue gas">flue gas</a> and dewatering lower rank coals (<a href="/wiki/Brown_coal" class="mw-redirect" title="Brown coal">brown coals</a>) to improve the <a href="/wiki/Calorific_value" class="mw-redirect" title="Calorific value">calorific value</a>, and thus the efficiency of the conversion into <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electricity</a>. Figures from the United States Environmental Protection Agency show that these technologies have made today's coal-based generating fleet 77 percent cleaner on the basis of regulated emissions per unit of energy produced.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<p>Clean coal technology usually addresses atmospheric problems resulting from burning coal. Historically, the primary focus was on SO<sub>2</sub> and NO<sub>x</sub>, the most important gases in causation of <a href="/wiki/Acid_rain" title="Acid rain">acid rain</a>, and particulates which cause visible air pollution and deleterious effects on human health. More recent focus has been on carbon dioxide (due to its impact on <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> and concern over toxic species such as mercury.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> Concerns exist regarding the economic viability of these technologies and the timeframe of delivery,<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> potentially high hidden economic costs in terms of social and environmental damage,<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> and the costs and viability of disposing of removed carbon and other toxic matter.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Oxyfuel_CCS_fossil_fuel_power_plant_operation.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Oxyfuel_CCS_fossil_fuel_power_plant_operation.png/220px-Oxyfuel_CCS_fossil_fuel_power_plant_operation.png" width="220" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Oxyfuel_CCS_fossil_fuel_power_plant_operation.png/330px-Oxyfuel_CCS_fossil_fuel_power_plant_operation.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Oxyfuel_CCS_fossil_fuel_power_plant_operation.png/440px-Oxyfuel_CCS_fossil_fuel_power_plant_operation.png 2x" data-file-width="466" data-file-height="302" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Oxyfuel_CCS_fossil_fuel_power_plant_operation.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An oxyfuel CCS power plant operation processes the exhaust gases so as to separate the CO<sub>2</sub> so that it may be stored or sequestered</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Several different technological methods are available for the purpose of carbon capture as demanded by the clean coal concept:</p>
<ul>
<li>Pre-combustion capture – This involves <a href="/wiki/Gasification" title="Gasification">gasification</a> of a feedstock (such as coal) to form synthesis gas, which may be <a href="/wiki/Water-gas_shift_reaction" title="Water-gas shift reaction">shifted</a> to produce a H<sub>2</sub> and CO<sub>2</sub>-rich gas mixture, from which the CO<sub>2</sub> can be efficiently captured and separated, transported, and ultimately sequestered,<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> This technology is usually associated with <a href="/wiki/Integrated_Gasification_Combined_Cycle" class="mw-redirect" title="Integrated Gasification Combined Cycle">Integrated Gasification Combined Cycle</a> process configurations.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup></li>
<li>Post-combustion capture – This refers to capture of CO<sub>2</sub> from exhaust gases of combustion processes, typically using sorbents, solvents, or membrane separations to remove CO<sub>2</sub> from the bulk gases.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Oxy-fuel_combustion" class="mw-redirect" title="Oxy-fuel combustion">Oxy-fuel combustion</a> – Fossil fuels such as coal are burned in a mixture of recirculated flue gas and oxygen, rather than in air, which largely eliminates nitrogen from the flue gas enabling efficient, low-cost CO<sub>2</sub> capture.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Kemper_Project" title="Kemper Project">Kemper County IGCC Project</a>, a 582 MW <a href="/wiki/Coal_gasification" title="Coal gasification">coal gasification</a>-based power plant, will use pre-combustion capture of CO<sub>2</sub> to capture 65% of the CO<sub>2</sub> the plant produces, which will be utilized/geologically sequestered in <a href="/wiki/Enhanced_oil_recovery" title="Enhanced oil recovery">enhanced oil recovery</a> operations.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Saskatchewan Government's <a href="/wiki/Boundary_Dam_Power_Station" title="Boundary Dam Power Station">Boundary Dam Power Station</a> Integrated Carbon Capture and Sequestration Demonstration Project will use post-combustion, amine-based scrubber technology to capture 90% of the CO<sub>2</sub> emitted by Unit 3 of the power plant; this CO<sub>2</sub> will be pipelined to and utilized for enhanced oil recovery in the Weyburn oil fields.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup></p>
<p>An early example of a coal-based plant using (oxy-fuel) carbon-capture technology is Swedish company <a href="/wiki/Vattenfall" title="Vattenfall">Vattenfall</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Schwarze_Pumpe_power_station" title="Schwarze Pumpe power station">Schwarze Pumpe power station</a> located in <a href="/wiki/Spremberg" title="Spremberg">Spremberg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, built by German firm <a href="/wiki/Siemens" title="Siemens">Siemens</a>, which went on-line in September 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-discovermagazine_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-discovermagazine-122">[122]</a></sup> The facility captures CO<sub>2</sub> and acid rain producing pollutants, separates them, and compresses the CO<sub>2</sub> into a liquid. Plans are to inject the CO<sub>2</sub> into depleted natural gas fields or other geological formations. Vattenfall opines that this technology is considered not to be a final solution for CO<sub>2</sub> reduction in the atmosphere, but provides an achievable solution in the near term while more desirable alternative solutions to power generation can be made economically practical.<sup id="cite_ref-discovermagazine_122-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-discovermagazine-122">[122]</a></sup> In 2014 research and development were discontinued due to high costs making the technology unviable.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Bioremediation">Bioremediation</span></h2>
<p>The white rot fungus <a href="/wiki/Trametes_versicolor" title="Trametes versicolor">Trametes versicolor</a> can grow on and metabolize naturally occurring coal.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> The bacteria <a href="/wiki/Diplococcus" title="Diplococcus">Diplococcus</a> has been found to degrade coal, raising its temperature.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_aspects">Economic aspects</span></h2>
<p>Coal (by liquefaction technology) is one of the <a href="/wiki/Backstop_resources" title="Backstop resources">backstop resources</a> that could limit escalation of <a href="/wiki/Oil_prices" class="mw-redirect" title="Oil prices">oil prices</a> and mitigate the effects of transportation energy shortage that will occur under <a href="/wiki/Peak_oil" title="Peak oil">peak oil</a>. This is contingent on liquefaction production capacity becoming large enough to satiate the very large and growing demand for petroleum. Estimates of the cost of producing liquid fuels from coal suggest that domestic U.S. production of fuel from coal becomes cost-competitive with oil priced at around $35 per barrel,<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> with the $35 being the break-even cost. With oil prices as low as around $40 per barrel in the U.S. as of December 2008, liquid coal lost some of its economic allure in the U.S., but will probably be re-vitalized, similar to <a href="/wiki/Oil_sand" class="mw-redirect" title="Oil sand">oil sand</a> projects, with an oil price around $70 per barrel.</p>
<p>In China, due to an increasing need for liquid energy in the transportation sector, coal liquefaction projects were given high priority even during periods of oil prices below $40 per barrel.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> This is probably because China prefers not to be dependent on foreign oil, instead utilizing its enormous domestic coal reserves. As oil prices were increasing during the first half of 2009, the coal liquefaction projects in China were again boosted, and these projects are profitable with an oil barrel price of $40.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup></p>
<p>China is the largest producer of coal in the world. It is the world's largest energy consumer, and relies on coal to supply 69% of its energy needs.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> An estimated 5 million people worked in China's coal-mining industry in 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
<p>Coal pollution costs the EU €43 billion each year.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> Measures to cut air pollution may have beneficial long-term economic impacts for individuals.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_density_and_carbon_impact">Energy density and carbon impact</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Energy_value_of_coal" title="Energy value of coal">Energy value of coal</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Energy_density" title="Energy density">energy density</a> of coal, i.e. its <a href="/wiki/Heating_value" class="mw-redirect" title="Heating value">heating value</a>, is roughly 24 <a href="/wiki/Megajoule" class="mw-redirect" title="Megajoule">megajoules</a> per kilogram<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> (approximately 6.7 <a href="/wiki/Kilowatt-hours" class="mw-redirect" title="Kilowatt-hours">kilowatt-hours</a> per kg). For a coal power plant with a 40% efficiency, it takes an estimated 325&#160;kg (717&#160;lb) of coal to power a 100&#160;W lightbulb for one year.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup></p>
<p>As of 2006, the average efficiency of electricity-generating power stations was 31%; in 2002, coal represented about 23% of total global energy supply, an equivalent of 3.4 billion tonnes of coal, of which 2.8 billion tonnes were used for electricity generation.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup></p>
<p>The US Energy Information Agency's 1999 report on CO<sub>2</sub> emissions for energy generation quotes an emission factor of 0.963&#160;kg CO<sub>2</sub>/kWh for coal power, compared to 0.881&#160;kg CO<sub>2</sub>/kWh (oil), or 0.569&#160;kg CO<sub>2</sub>/kWh (natural gas).<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Underground_fires">Underground fires</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Coal_seam_fire" title="Coal seam fire">Coal seam fire</a></div>
<p>Thousands of coal fires are burning around the world.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> Those burning underground can be difficult to locate and many cannot be extinguished. Fires can cause the ground above to subside, their combustion gases are dangerous to life, and breaking out to the surface can initiate surface <a href="/wiki/Wildfire" title="Wildfire">wildfires</a>. Coal seams can be set on fire by <a href="/wiki/Spontaneous_combustion" title="Spontaneous combustion">spontaneous combustion</a> or contact with a <a href="/wiki/Mine_fire" class="mw-redirect" title="Mine fire">mine fire</a> or surface fire. Lightning strikes are an important source of ignition. The coal continues to burn slowly back into the seam until oxygen (air) can no longer reach the flame front. A grass fire in a coal area can set dozens of coal seams on fire.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> Coal fires in China burn an estimated 120 million tons of coal a year, emitting 360 million metric tons of CO<sub>2</sub>, amounting to 2–3% of the annual worldwide production of CO<sub>2</sub> from <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuels</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Centralia,_Pennsylvania" title="Centralia, Pennsylvania">Centralia, Pennsylvania</a> (a <a href="/wiki/Borough_(Pennsylvania)" title="Borough (Pennsylvania)">borough</a> located in the <a href="/wiki/Coal_Region" title="Coal Region">Coal Region</a> of the United States), an exposed vein of anthracite ignited in 1962 due to a trash fire in the borough landfill, located in an abandoned <a href="/wiki/Anthracite_coal" class="mw-redirect" title="Anthracite coal">anthracite</a> <a href="/wiki/Surface_mining" title="Surface mining">strip mine</a> pit. Attempts to extinguish the fire were unsuccessful, and it continues to burn underground to this day. The Australian <a href="/wiki/Burning_Mountain" title="Burning Mountain">Burning Mountain</a> was originally believed to be a volcano, but the smoke and ash come from a coal fire that has been burning for some 6,000 years.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup></p>
<p>At <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kuh_i_Malik&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Kuh i Malik (page does not exist)">Kuh i Malik</a> in <a href="/wiki/Yagnob_Valley" class="mw-redirect" title="Yagnob Valley">Yagnob Valley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tajikistan" title="Tajikistan">Tajikistan</a>, coal deposits have been burning for thousands of years, creating vast underground labyrinths full of unique minerals, some of them very beautiful. Local people once used this method<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (December 2014)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> to mine <a href="/wiki/Ammoniac" class="mw-redirect" title="Ammoniac">ammoniac</a>. This place has been well-known since the time of <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a>, but European geographers misinterpreted the Ancient Greek descriptions as the evidence of active <a href="/wiki/Volcanism" title="Volcanism">volcanism</a> in <a href="/wiki/Turkestan" title="Turkestan">Turkestan</a> (up to the 19th century, when the Russian army invaded the area).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The reddish siltstone rock that caps many ridges and buttes in the <a href="/wiki/Powder_River_Basin" title="Powder River Basin">Powder River Basin</a> in <a href="/wiki/Wyoming" title="Wyoming">Wyoming</a> and in western <a href="/wiki/North_Dakota" title="North Dakota">North Dakota</a> is called <i>porcelanite</i>, which resembles the coal burning waste "clinker" or volcanic "<a href="/wiki/Scoria" title="Scoria">scoria</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> Clinker is rock that has been fused by the natural burning of coal. In the Powder River Basin approximately 27 to 54 billion tons of coal burned within the past three million years.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> Wild coal fires in the area were reported by the <a href="/wiki/Lewis_and_Clark_Expedition" title="Lewis and Clark Expedition">Lewis and Clark Expedition</a> as well as explorers and settlers in the area.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World_coal_reserves">World coal reserves</span></h3>
<p>The 948 billion short tons of recoverable coal reserves estimated by the Energy Information Administration are equal to about 4,196 BBOE (billion <a href="/wiki/Barrel_of_oil_equivalent" title="Barrel of oil equivalent">barrels of oil equivalent</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-EIAreserves_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EIAreserves-39">[39]</a></sup> The amount of coal burned during 2007 was estimated at 7.075 billion short tons, or 133.179 quadrillion BTU's.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> This is an average of 18.8 million BTU per short ton. In terms of heat content, this is about 57,000,000 barrels (9,100,000&#160;m<sup>3</sup>) of oil equivalent per day. By comparison in 2007, natural gas provided 51,000,000 barrels (8,100,000&#160;m<sup>3</sup>) of oil equivalent per day, while oil provided 85,800,000 barrels (13,640,000&#160;m<sup>3</sup>) per day.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/BP" title="BP">British Petroleum</a>, in its 2007 report, estimated at 2006 end that there were 147 years <a href="/wiki/Reserves-to-production_ratio" title="Reserves-to-production ratio">reserves-to-production ratio</a> based on <i>proven</i> coal reserves worldwide. This figure only includes reserves classified as "proven"; exploration drilling programs by mining companies, particularly in under-explored areas, are continually providing new reserves. In many cases, companies are aware of coal deposits that have not been sufficiently drilled to qualify as "proven". However, some nations haven't updated their information and assume reserves remain at the same levels even with withdrawals.</p>
<p>Of the three fossil fuels, coal has the most widely distributed reserves; coal is mined in over 100 countries, and on all continents except <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a>. The largest reserves are found in the United States, Russia, China, Australia and India. Note the table below.</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Major_coal_producers">Major coal producers</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_coal_production" title="List of countries by coal production">List of countries by coal production</a></div>
<p>The reserve life is an estimate based only on current production levels and proved reserves level for the countries shown, and makes no assumptions of future production or even current production trends. Countries with annual production higher than 100 million tonnes are shown. For comparison, data for the European Union is also shown. Shares are based on data expressed in tonnes oil equivalent.</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Major_coal_exporters">Major coal exporters</span></h3>
<p>Countries with annual gross export higher than 10 million tonnes are shown. In terms of net export the largest exporters are still <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> (328.1 millions tonnes), <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a> (316.2) and <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> (100.2).</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Major_coal_importers">Major coal importers</span></h3>
<p>Countries with annual gross import higher than 20 million tonnes are shown. In terms of net import the largest importers are still <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> (206.0 millions tonnes), <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> (172.4) and <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a> (125.8).<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_usage">Cultural usage</span></h2>
<p>Coal is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_U.S._state_minerals,_rocks,_stones_and_gemstones" title="List of U.S. state minerals, rocks, stones and gemstones">official state mineral</a> of <a href="/wiki/Kentucky" title="Kentucky">Kentucky</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Kentucky_mineral_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kentucky_mineral-154">[154]</a></sup> and the official state rock of <a href="/wiki/Utah" title="Utah">Utah</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-Utah_rock_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Utah_rock-155">[155]</a></sup> both <a href="/wiki/U.S._state" title="U.S. state">U.S. states</a> have a historic link to coal mining.</p>
<p>Some cultures hold that children who misbehave will receive only a lump of coal from <a href="/wiki/Santa_Claus" title="Santa Claus">Santa Claus</a> for Christmas in their <a href="/wiki/Christmas_stocking" title="Christmas stocking">christmas stockings</a> instead of presents.</p>
<p>It is also customary and considered lucky in Scotland and the North of England to give coal as a gift on New Year's Day. This occurs as part of <a href="/wiki/First-Foot" class="mw-redirect" title="First-Foot">First-Footing</a> and represents warmth for the year to come.</p>
